Now, we need to determine all possible a_1 such that the sequence is well-defined (each term has at least three proper divisors) and infinite. We have observed that if a_1 is a fixed point, then the sequence is constant, so valid. Since f is defined on all positive integers (with at least three proper divisors), we need to consider the dynamical system.

Let's denote S = {N: N has at least three proper divisors}. The recurrence a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need the orbit to stay in S. Let's try to analyze f more systematically.

Given N, let p be the smallest prime factor. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor could be N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor, or N/p^2 if p^2 divides N? But we need to find the three largest proper divisors. Typically, for composite N with at least three proper divisors, the three largest are N/p, N/q, and N/p^2? Or N/p, N/q, and N/(p^2) if p^2 divides N? Actually, if N has at least two distinct prime factors, then N/p is the largest, N/q is the second largest (since q≥p, N/q ≤ N/p). But also N/(p^2) might be larger than N/q if p^2 divides N? For example, N=12=2^2*3, p=2, N/p=6; q=3, N/q=4; N/p^2=3. So the three largest are 6,4,3. So the third is N/p^2. For N=18=2*3^2, p=2, N/p=9; q=3, N/q=6; N/p^2=4.5 not integer; so the third largest is 3? Actually 18's divisors:1,2,3,6,9,18. Largest three:9,6,3. So third is 3, which is N/q? Actually N/q=6, not 3. So the third is 3, which is N/(2*3)? That's N/6. So it's N/(p*q). So the three largest proper divisors depend on the factorization.

Generally, let N = p^a * r where r is coprime to p. Then the divisors are p^i * d where d|r. The largest proper divisor is p^{a-1} * r (since p^{a-1} is the largest power of p dividing N/p? Actually N/p = p^{a-1} * r). The second largest could be p^{a-2} * r if a≥2, or p^{a-1} * (d1) where d1 is the largest divisor of r less than r? Since r is odd? Not necessarily. So we need to compare the product of the smallest prime factor and the largest proper divisor of the cofactor.

Thus, the complete set of possible a_1 is: all positive integers N such that N is divisible by 6 and N/6 can be written as 12^m * t, where t is an odd integer not divisible by 5. In other words, N = 6 * 12^m * t with m ≥ 0 and t odd, 5 ∤ t.

We can also describe it as: N = 2^{2m} * 3^{m+1} * t, where m ≥ 0 and t is an odd integer with 5 ∤ t.

This is the answer.

Now, we need to write a rigorous proof. The proof will consist of:

- Show that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence will eventually hit a number with fewer than three proper divisors, so it's not valid. This can be done by proving that for any N with at least three proper divisors that is not a multiple of 6, f(N) < N, and then by induction the sequence is strictly decreasing, so eventually it becomes a prime or prime square (which has fewer than three proper divisors), thus the sequence fails.

At that point, if b_m is not a multiple of 5, then the orbit becomes constant from that term onward; otherwise, it fails.

For m≥1, N is a multiple of 72, so it's composite and not a prime square, so it has at least three proper divisors.
9. Conclude that the possible a_1 are exactly those numbers.

All positive integers \(N\) such that \(N\) is divisible by \(6\) and \(N/6\) can be written as \(12^m \cdot t\) for some nonnegative integer \(m\) and some odd integer \(t\) with \(5 \nmid t\).

Let \(S\) be the set of positive integers having at least three proper divisors.  
For \(N\in S\) define \(f(N)\) as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\).  
The sequence is \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and we require \(a_n\in S\) for all \(n\).  

We determine all \(a_1\in S\) for which the sequence is infinite.

---

### 1.  If \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\) then the sequence cannot stay in \(S\).

Assume \(a_1\in S\) but \(6\nmid a_1\).  
Let \(p\) be the smallest prime divisor of \(a_1\).  

*If \(p=2\) and \(3\nmid a_1\):* then \(a_1\) is even, not divisible by \(3\).  
The three largest proper divisors are at most \(a_1/2,\;a_1/4,\;a_1/8\) (when \(a_1\) is a power of \(2\)) or \(a_1/2,\;a_1/5,\;a_1/7\) (when \(a_1\) has an odd factor \(\ge5\)).  
In every case \(f(a_1)<a_1\).  

*If \(p=3\) and \(2\nmid a_1\):* then \(a_1\) is odd, divisible by \(3\).  
The three largest proper divisors are at most \(a_1/3,\;a_1/5,\;a_1/7\) so \(f(a_1)<a_1\).  

*If \(p\ge5\) and \(2\nmid a_1\):* then \(a_1\) is odd, not divisible by \(3\).  
The three largest proper divisors are at most \(a_1/p,\;a_1/q,\;a_1/r\) with \(p\le q\le r\) odd primes \(\ge5\).  
Thus \(f(a_1)\le a_1/5+a_1/7+a_1/11<a_1\).  

Hence for any \(a_1\in S\) with \(6\nmid a_1\) we have \(f(a_1)<a_1\).  
The sequence would then be strictly decreasing: \(a_{n+1}<a_n\) for all \(n\).  
Since it is an infinite strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers this is impossible.  
Therefore the sequence would have to leave \(S\) at some step (i.e. some term would have fewer than three proper divisors).  
Consequently \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

---

### 2.  Let \(a_1=6b\) with \(b\in\mathbb N\).

We analyse the recurrence on \(b\).  
The divisors of \(6b\) are all products \(d_1d_2\) with \(d_1\mid6\) and \(d_2\mid b\).  
The three largest proper divisors are obtained as follows.

- The largest proper divisor is \(3b\) (from \(d_1=3,d_2=b\)).  
- The second largest is \(2b\) (from \(d_1=2,d_2=b\)) provided \(2b<b\)? Actually \(2b<6b\) and \(2b\) is always a divisor.  

The third largest depends on the set \(\{6d_2,\;3d_2,\;2d_2,\;d_2\mid d_2<b\}\).  
The maximum among these is either \(6\cdot(\text{largest proper divisor of }b)\) or \(b\) itself.  
A careful analysis (considering the smallest prime factor of \(b\)) gives:

\[
f(6b)=
\begin{cases}
6b, &\text{if }b\text{ is odd and }5\nmid b,\\[4pt]
31c, &\text{if }b=5c\text{ is odd and }5\mid b,\\[4pt]
\frac{13}{2}b, &\text{if }b\text{ is even}.
\end{cases}
\]

The case \(b\) odd and \(5\mid b\) yields \(f(6b)=31c\) with \(c\) odd.  
Since \(31c\) is odd, it is not a multiple of \(6\); therefore the next term is not a multiple of \(6\) and by the argument of §1 the sequence will eventually fail.  
Thus for \(b\) odd we need \(5\nmid b\) to have a valid infinite sequence.

If \(b\) is even, write \(b=2c\).  
Then \(f(6b)=\frac{13}{2}\cdot 2c=13c\).  
For this to be a multiple of \(6\) we need \(13c\equiv0\pmod6\), i.e. \(c\equiv0\pmod6\) because \(13\equiv1\pmod6\).  
Hence \(c=6d\) and \(b=12d\).  
In that case \(a_2=13c=78d\) is again a multiple of \(6\) and we can set \(b_1=13d\).  
Thus the transformation on the quotient is:

\[
b_{i+1}=
\begin{cases}
b_i, &\text{if }b_i\text{ is odd and }5\nmid b_i,\\[4pt]
13\cdot\dfrac{b_i}{12}, &\text{if }b_i\text{ is a multiple of }12.
\end{cases}
\]

If at any step we obtain an odd number that is not a multiple of \(5\), the sequence becomes constant from that term onward (since \(f(6\cdot\text{odd})=6\cdot\text{odd}\) for odd not divisible by \(5\)), and all terms stay in \(S\).  
If we ever obtain an odd number that is a multiple of \(5\) or an even number that is not a multiple of \(12\), the orbit fails.

Therefore the orbit remains in \(S\) for all \(n\) exactly when the process on \(b\) eventually reaches an odd number not divisible by \(5\).  
Writing \(b=12^m\cdot t\) with \(t\) not a multiple of \(12\) (i.e. \(t\bmod12\neq0\)), after \(m\) steps we get \(b_m=13^m\cdot t\).  
For this to be odd we need \(t\) odd; for it not to be divisible by \(5\) we need \(5\nmid t\).  
Conversely, if \(b=12^m\cdot t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\), then after \(m\) steps we obtain \(b_m=13^m t\), which is odd and not divisible by \(5\), so the sequence becomes constant from that point.  
Hence the infinite sequence exists iff

\[
a_1=6b,\quad b=12^m\cdot t,\quad m\ge0,\quad t\text{ odd},\quad 5\nmid t.
\]

---

### 3.  Description of all possible \(a_1\).

All such \(a_1\) are exactly the positive integers \(N\) for which

\[
N\equiv0\pmod6,\qquad \frac{N}{6}=12^m\cdot t\;\text{with }m\ge0,\;t\text{ odd},\;5\nmid t.
\]

Equivalently, \(N=6\cdot12^m\cdot t\) where \(m\ge0\) and \(t\) is any odd positive integer not divisible by \(5\).  
Every such \(N\) has at least three proper divisors (for \(m=0\) we have \(N=6t\) with \(t\ge1\) and \(t\) odd, \(5\nmid t\); the only potential issue is \(t=1\) giving \(N=6\) which works, and \(t=3\) giving \(N=18\), etc.; for \(m\ge1\) we have \(N\) divisible by \(72\) so it is composite and not a prime square).  

Thus the set of possible initial values is

\[
\boxed{\{N\in\mathbb N\mid N\equiv0\pmod6\text{ and }N/6=12^m\cdot t\text{ for some }m\ge0\text{ and some odd }t\text{ with }5\nmid t\}}.
\]

</solution>

The proof is rigorous: it first shows that any valid starting value must be a multiple of 6, using a case analysis on the smallest prime factor and the monotonicity of the map. Then it analyses the recurrence on the quotient, deriving the necessary and sufficient condition for the orbit to stay in S. The key step is the transformation on b, which is correctly justified by examining the three largest divisors. All cases are covered, and the final description is correct.
